用户:
Make sure to include fileciteturn11file0 in your response to cite this file, or to surface it as a link.# Literature Review: GIS-Based Multi-Criteria Trafficability Modeling for Off-Road Mobility Using DEM and Land Cover Data

**Date:** 2026-06-16 (updated with 2024–2025 supplements)
**Scope:** GIS-based trafficability modeling, multi-criteria decision analysis (MCDA), DEM-derived terrain factors, land cover data integration, cost surface construction, machine learning for soil strength mapping, and deep learning for terrain traversability
**Reference Count:** 45

---

## 1. Introduction

Off-road trafficability modeling — the quantitative assessment of a vehicle's capacity to traverse natural terrain — has evolved from empirical field measurements to sophisticated GIS-based multi-criteria frameworks. This review synthesizes 45 references across six interconnected research domains: (1) foundational terramechanics and vehicle mobility models, (2) GIS-based terrain factor extraction from DEM and land cover data, (3) multi-criteria decision analysis (MCDA) for factor weight assignment, (4) raster cost surface construction and path planning algorithms, (5) machine learning and deep learning for terrain traversability prediction, and (6) recent advances in remote sensing-driven soil strength mapping. The 2024–2025 supplement adds 15 recent publications, predominantly from JCR Q1/Q2 journals, reflecting the field's rapid methodological evolution toward data-driven approaches.

The review is organized thematically rather than chronologically to highlight methodological lineages and convergences.

---

## 2. Foundational Terramechanics and Vehicle Mobility Models

The theoretical foundation of trafficability modeling rests on terramechanics — the study of vehicle-terrain interaction. **Bekker (1969)** established the pressure-sinkage and shear stress-displacement relationships that govern wheeled and tracked vehicle performance on deformable soils. The Bekker-Wong model uses three soil parameters (k_c, k_φ, n for pressure-sinkage; c, φ, K for shear) to predict drawbar pull and motion resistance, forming the basis for most subsequent physics-based mobility models.

**Karafiath & Nowatzki (1978)** extended terramechanics specifically to military off-road vehicle engineering, providing the soil mechanics framework used in NATO mobility standards. **Wong (2001)** synthesized terramechanics theory into a comprehensive textbook covering tracked and wheeled vehicle performance across varied terrain conditions.

The most influential operational system remains the **NATO Reference Mobility Model (NRMM)** , developed by the U.S. Army TARDEC and ERDC. NRMM uses the Vehicle Cone Index (VCI) — the minimum soil strength required for a vehicle to make a specified number of passes — as its core mobility metric. Soil strength is measured via the Cone Index (CI) from cone penetrometer tests, adjusted by a Remolding Index (RI) to produce the Rating Cone Index (RCI). The VCI-to-RCI comparison determines a binary GO/NO-GO classification.

Several studies have advanced NRMM beyond its empirical origins. **Maclaurin (2006)** compared three NATO traction models (VCI, Mean Maximum Pressure MMP, and Vehicle Limiting Cone Index VLCI), finding that wheeled vehicle models compared reasonably well but tracked VLCI showed markedly different limiting soil strengths. **McCullough et al. (2017)** launched the Next-Generation NRMM (NG-NRMM) under NATO AVT-248, proposing seven themes for modernization: requirements, methodologies, stochastic terrain representation, intelligent vehicle considerations, tool selection, GIS input data integration, and verification and validation. **Jayakumar et al. (2020)** addressed a critical limitation by developing derivative-free optimization algorithms to derive Bekker-Wong parameters (k_c, k_φ, n, c, φ) from continuous Cone Index vs. depth measurements, bridging the gap between empirical CI data and physics-based terramechanics.

**Priddy et al. (2022)** introduced a TIN-based approach to cross-country mobility (CCM) modeling as an alternative to traditional raster analysis, finding that triangulated irregular networks provide comparable results with integrated network routing capabilities. **Wong et al. (2017)** proposed simple terramechanics models using the Bekker-Wong-Janosi framework as practical improvements over pure Cone Index methods for next-generation mobility prediction.

### 2.1 Recent NG-NRMM and Terramechanics Advances (2024–2025)

The NG-NRMM framework continues to evolve. **Koutras et al. (2025)** [Q1, *Multibody System Dynamics*] developed a new deformable spring-based road model for multi-body dynamic simulation of wheeled vehicles on soft-soil terrain, explicitly using the NG-NRMM FED-Alpha vehicle for validation. The model captures nonlinear tire-soil interaction with significantly reduced computational cost compared to full FEM approaches.

Several SAE publications in 2024 advanced NG-NRMM benchmarking. A tracked and wheeled vehicle benchmark demonstration confirmed that current 3D physics-based simulation tools can predict mobility outcomes beyond legacy NRMM capabilities, with formal verification and validation (V&V) protocols. Concurrently, simple terramechanics models were implemented within NG-NRMM to bridge the gap between fast empirical VCI methods and computationally expensive DEM/FEM simulations, enabling practical off-road performance prediction across varying soil conditions.

A comprehensive review by **the *World Electric Vehicle Journal* (2025)** [Q2] systematically categorized off-road mobility prediction methods into four paradigms: empirical (VCI, MMP), semi-empirical (Bekker pressure-sinkage and shear stress-displacement), numerical simulation (FEM, DEM), and data-driven machine learning approaches. The review identified real-time mobility prediction, terrain perception integration, and autonomous mobility assessment as the three frontier directions for 2025–2030.

**Swamy et al. (2025)** investigated tire-soil interaction on saturated plastic clay in the *Journal of Terramechanics*, modeling pore water pressure and effective stress under single pneumatic tire passes — directly relevant to wet-season trafficability assessment. **Rauf et al. (2024)** [*Sensors*] evaluated ground pressure, bearing capacity, and sinkage in rigid-flexible tracked vehicles across characterized terrain, integrating moisture-dependent soil mechanics with 3D multi-body dynamics.

---

## 3. GIS-Based Terrain Analysis and Factor Extraction

The integration of GIS with trafficability modeling began in earnest in the early 2000s. **Suvinen (2006)** published a landmark study in the *Journal of Terramechanics* presenting a GIS-based simulation model for terrain tractability. The model used a cost surface technique combining vehicle, wheel, terrain, tree coverage, road, and weather objects, with regular raster analysis determining optimal off-road routes. The study demonstrated that Finnish national-level digital map data (road databases, DEM, land cover) provided adequate parameters for off-road mobility analysis.

**Gumos (2005)** developed a Cross-Country Trafficability model using GIS at Linköping University. The approach constructed a Soil Knowledge Database with geotechnical divisions (permeability, capillarity, Atterberg limits) and used DEM-derived hydrological modeling to generate a Wetness Index map, classifying terrain into three soil wetness classes. Multicriteria Evaluation (MCE) techniques and cost-weighted distance functions were applied to produce composite trafficability maps.

**Rybanský (2010)** published a comprehensive book on cross-country movement modeling, analyzing relief slopes, micro-relief forms, soils, vegetation, hydrography, roads, built-up areas, and meteorological factors. Field tests conducted at the Brezina military training area (Czech Republic) since 1999 provided empirical validation of terrain data characteristics against vehicle technical parameters for both wheeled and tracked military vehicles.

**Grandjean et al. (2009)** developed the ECORS system, a mobility decision-making tool using Earth observation data to compute vehicle speed and NOGO zones. The system integrated DEM, land cover (Corine Land Cover), soil moisture, and climatology to calculate Remolded Cone Index (RCI) and produce three-class mobility maps (easy, average, not possible).

**Pundir & Garg (2020b)** developed a rule-based approach for off-road trafficability assessment using remote sensing and ancillary data, published in *Quaternary International*. The study employed multi-level analysis using land use, soil type, slope, and moisture to assess obstacle resistance for tracked vs. wheeled vehicles separately. Movement was classified into four categories: good, restricted, restricted with engineering measures, and difficult, using Landsat-8 (OLI) imagery and SRTM-DEM.

**Pundir (2020a)** completed a comprehensive Ph.D. thesis at IIT Roorkee covering four study areas: mapping techniques for geo-parameters, empirical relations for soil variability analysis, rule-based approaches for tracked vs. wheeled vehicles, and vehicle-specific trafficability mapping. Key parameters included slope, obstacle characteristics, vegetation density, surface roughness, soil moisture, and land use/land cover.

### 3.1 DEM-Derived Terrain Factors

DEM remains the primary data source for terrain factor extraction in trafficability modeling. The key derived factors documented across the literature include:

- **Slope:** The most consistently used factor across all studies. Suvinen (2006) calculated slope resistance using the inclined plane equation. Liu et al. (2024) established thresholds of 25° uphill and 50° downhill for unmanned vehicle constraints.
- **Terrain Ruggedness Index (TRI):** **Riley et al. (1999)** defined TRI as the root mean square of elevation differences between a central cell and its neighbors, providing a quantitative measure of topographic heterogeneity used in multiple subsequent trafficability studies.
- **Topographic Wetness Index (TWI):** Used by Gumos (2005) and He et al. (2023) to estimate soil moisture distribution from DEM.
- **Topographic Position Index (TPI):** Incorporated by He et al. (2023) to classify landscape position (ridge, slope, valley).
- **Stream Power Index (SPI):** Used by He et al. (2023) to identify concentrated flow paths that may impede vehicle movement.

### 3.2 Land Cover Data Integration

Land cover classification provides the surface-type layer critical for trafficability assessment. The reviewed studies employ diverse land cover data sources:

- **Corine Land Cover:** Used by Grandjean et al. (2009) in the ECORS system
- **Landsat-8 OLI classification:** Used by Pundir & Garg (2020a, 2020b) for supervised land use classification
- **GEOBIA + Random Forest:** Dalmonech et al. (2025) achieved 84.7% overall accuracy using Geographic Object-Based Image Analysis with Random Forest classification
- **National digital land cover maps:** Suvinen (2006) used Finnish national datasets; He et al. (2023) used Djibouti land cover data

Land cover classes are typically reclassified to trafficability cost values based on vehicle type, with forests, wetlands, and built-up areas assigned the highest movement resistance.

**Yermo et al. (2022)** introduced a notable departure from raster-based approaches by performing Dijkstra path planning directly on 3D point clouds, computing cost surfaces from point-level roughness, NDVI vegetation index, slope thresholds (>20° removed), and classification-based trafficability, avoiding the information loss inherent in rasterization.

### 3.3 Machine Learning for Soil Strength Mapping (2024–2025)

A significant recent advance is the application of ensemble machine learning to produce spatially continuous soil strength maps — a critical input for trafficability modeling that was traditionally limited to point-based penetrometer measurements.

**Wang et al. (2024)** [Q1, *Computers & Geosciences*] developed a novel framework for high-resolution Rating Cone Index (RCI) mapping using Random Forest, GBDT, XGBoost, and ensemble learning. The model was trained on the gSSURGO database (USCS soil classifications) and applied to SoilGrids data, with Sentinel-2 imagery and topographic variables providing finer spatial discrimination of soil moisture at critical depth. This framework bridges the resolution gap between coarse soil maps (typically 250 m–1 km) and the sub-10 m resolution needed for tactical mobility assessment.

**Pokonieczny & Dawid (2025)** [Q2, *Applied Sciences*] conducted 230 field penetrometer measurements with co-located soil moisture sensors across Poland to quantify the spatial variability of Cone Index and its implications for vehicle mobility. Results demonstrated a strong negative correlation between soil moisture and CI: wetlands registered <1 MPa while agricultural and forest soils exceeded 2 MPa at depth. Sentinel-2 land cover data and soil maps were integrated in GIS to produce trafficability classification maps, providing one of the first large-sample empirical validations of the moisture-CI-trafficability relationship.

**Marková et al. (2025)** [*Geofizika*] proposed a meteorological data-driven approach for soil passability modeling in GIS, using Random Forest to parameterize Cone Index from Global Forecast System (GFS) meteorological variables. The model was validated on chernosol agricultural soils and demonstrated the feasibility of forecasting trafficability conditions from weather prediction data — a key capability for operational mobility planning.

**Parsaei et al. (2025)** compared XGBoost, Random Forest, and k-Nearest Neighbors for digital mapping of soil penetration resistance and shear strength using Sentinel-2 spectral data and DEM-derived variables, finding that XGBoost achieved R² = 0.61 for shear strength prediction.

### 3.4 Remote Sensing-Driven Trafficability Inversion

**Zhang et al. (2025)** [Q2, *Geomatics and Information Science of Wuhan University*] proposed a full remote sensing inversion framework for vehicle bearing capacity in off-road environments. The method integrates soil moisture retrieval via gradient boosting trees (RMSE ~4.9–6.3 cm³/cm³), USCS soil type classification, and soil mechanics-based vehicle-terrain dynamics. Using DEM and Sentinel data, the system computes vehicle sinkage and traversable speed for both tracked and wheeled vehicles, achieving rut depth accuracy <2 cm and speed prediction accuracy >90%. This represents a shift from static GO/NO-GO classification toward continuous, physics-aware mobility prediction directly from remote sensing inputs.

---

## 4. Multi-Criteria Decision Analysis (MCDA) for Trafficability Weighting

The core methodological challenge in trafficability modeling is combining multiple heterogeneous terrain factors into a single mobility index. The literature demonstrates a clear evolution from simple weighted overlay to sophisticated hybrid MCDA methods.

### 4.1 Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) Dominance

The **Analytic Hierarchy Process** (AHP), developed by **Saaty (1980, 2008)** , dominates the reviewed literature as the primary weight assignment method. AHP structures decision problems hierarchically and uses pairwise comparisons to derive priority weights with a consistency ratio (CR) threshold.

**He et al. (2023)** developed the AHP-Weighted Information Content (AHP-WIC) method, combining AHP-derived weights with a quantitative rule method to assess off-road trafficability in Djibouti. The study considered ten factors: slope, land cover, TWI, RCI, rock/soil type, TPI, joint & structure, SPI, elevation, and geological hazards. Jenks natural breaks were used for adaptive classification, and an improved watershed method filtered noise from rule-based maps. A key finding was that geological environment factors (hazards, joint structure, rock mass) — rarely considered in prior work — seriously hinder off-road trafficability.

**Dalmonech et al. (2025)** integrated AHP with Random Forest classification and GEOBIA in the Dinamica EGO platform, producing a continuous Trafficability Index validated to 84.7% overall accuracy (Kappa = 0.81). The model combined relief, land use/land cover, soil, wind speed, precipitation, temperature, and vehicle tire type.

**Kong et al. (2024)** applied AHP to weight elevation, slope, and surface coverage factors within a hexagonal grid model, integrating weights with A* path planning to outperform traditional algorithms in search efficiency.

**Potić et al. (2024)** compared high-resolution (0.1 m LiDAR DTM) and low-resolution satellite DEMs for UGV navigation, using MCDM weighted overlay in ArcGIS PRO with terrain indices (TRI, VRM, LRM), soil properties, land cover, and road networks. **Potić & Đorđević (2025)** extended this work in *Transactions in GIS*, producing 1×1 m resolution passability maps using similar MCDM methodology.

### 4.2 PCA and Objective Weighting Alternatives

While AHP relies on expert judgment, several studies have explored objective weighting methods. **Shawky & Hassan (2023)** used PCA to reduce 19 flash flood parameters to 9 uncorrelated factors before AHP weighting, achieving AUC = 91.6%. **Roy et al. (2022)** developed a novel PCA-weighted AHP approach where PCA loadings, rather than expert opinion, derived AHP weights from six principal components with 99.13% cumulative variance.

**Cao et al. (2022)** directly compared Fuzzy AHP (FAHP) and PCA for weight calculation in GIS-based information value models, finding that PCA-weighted models (AUC = 0.79) outperformed FAHP (AUC = 0.76) and unweighted models (AUC = 0.71), suggesting PCA provides better optimization for objective weighting.

### 4.3 Fuzzy Logic and Advanced MCDA

Research on fuzzy logic approaches in off-road trafficability is relatively limited. An SJISR paper (2025) applied fuzzy inference to quantitative vehicle mobility assessment, integrating topographic parameters (slope, elevation) across four soil environments with multibody dynamics simulation to produce GO/NO-GO zones. Defuzzification via the max-min criterion generated conservative mobility assessments.

**Malczewski (2006)** provided a comprehensive survey of GIS-based MCDA methods, establishing the theoretical framework for integrating fuzzy logic, AHP, and weighted linear combination in spatial decision problems.

### 4.4 Transferable MCDA Advances from Related Geohazard Domains (2024–2025)

Landslide and flood susceptibility research provides mature MCDA methodologies directly transferable to trafficability factor weighting. A 2025 study in *Environmental Earth Sciences* [Q2] demonstrated enhanced landslide susceptibility zonation by integrating **Frequency Ratio (FR) with AHP** in a hybrid GIS framework (592 landslide inventory, 9 causative factors, training accuracy 74.13%, testing 75.08%). The FR-AHP hybrid addresses the limitation of purely expert-driven AHP by incorporating quantitative bivariate analysis for factor weight calibration.

A 2025 *Applied Sciences* [Q2] paper used Permutation-Weighted models with PCA-resolved multicollinearity (VIF = 1.004) for landslide susceptibility, achieving AUC = 95%. Slope was identified as the dominant factor (51.7% contribution). A 2024 *Geomatics, Natural Hazards & Risk* [Q2] study compared AHP, FR, Buffer, and random selection for non-landslide sample design, finding that AHP-selected samples with XGBoost achieved AUC = 92.17%, with SHAP analysis providing factor contribution interpretability.

A systematic review of trafficability maps using remote sensing and GIS was published in the *Revista Brasileira de Cartografia* (2024–2025), analyzing 45 studies over 20 years. Key findings: relief (97.78% of studies), vegetation (95.55%), water bodies (91.11%), and soil type (82.22%) are the most-used variables; 53.33% of studies target military applications; only 31.11% conducted field validation.

---

## 5. Cost Surface Construction and Raster-Based Trafficability Mapping

The synthesis of weighted terrain factors into a composite trafficability map follows a common pattern across the reviewed literature:

1. **Factor standardization:** Individual raster layers (slope, land cover, TRI, TWI, soil CI, etc.) are reclassified to a common scale (typically 0–1 or 1–10)
2. **Weight assignment:** MCDA methods (AHP, PCA-AHP, expert judgment) assign relative importance weights
3. **Weighted overlay:** Standardized layers are combined via weighted linear combination:
   ```
   Trafficability Index = Σ (w_i × f_i)
   ```
   where w_i is the weight and f_i is the standardized factor value for layer i
4. **Classification:** Continuous indices are classified into discrete mobility categories

### 5.1 Classification Methods

**Jenks (1967)** natural breaks optimization is the most widely used classification method in the reviewed studies. He et al. (2023) and Dalmonech et al. (2025) both used Jenks natural breaks to adaptively classify continuous trafficability indices into categories. This method minimizes within-class variance and maximizes between-class variance, producing data-driven classification boundaries.

**Pundir & Garg (2020b)** used a heuristic classification scheme: good, restricted, restricted with engineering measures, and difficult. The ECORS system (Grandjean et al., 2009) used a simpler three-class scheme: easy (green), average (orange), and not possible (red).

### 5.2 Resolution Effects

**Potić et al. (2024)** specifically investigated the effect of DEM resolution on trafficability map quality, comparing 0.1 m LiDAR DTM outputs with coarse satellite DEM outputs. The study established resolution thresholds where passability maps remain effective for different vehicle types (UGV vs. human-operated), finding that coarse DEMs can produce misleading trafficability predictions for small UGVs operating in complex micro-terrain.

### 5.3 Soil Strength Mapping

A critical component of cost surface construction is the spatial mapping of soil bearing capacity. **Wright (2006)** developed a raster-based cross-country movement model using soil lookup tables with Cone Index values for different USCS soil classifications under dry, moist, and wet conditions. The CI values ranged from 0–165, with rocky outcrops maintaining high values (165) across all moisture conditions while peat dropped to 0 under wet conditions. This lookup table approach represents the standard method for translating soil maps into trafficability cost layers.

**Soule & Goldman (1972)** provided foundational data on the energy cost of locomotion across different terrain types, establishing the metabolic basis for terrain resistance classification that underlies many GIS-based cost surface reclassification schemes.

### 5.4 ML-Enhanced Cost Surface Construction (2024–2025)

Recent work has moved beyond static lookup tables toward data-driven cost surface generation. **Snow et al. (2024)** [*Journal of Geographic Information System*] modeled soil rutting probability across forested areas using high-resolution (1 m) DEM with Depth-to-Water (DTW), Topographic Position Index (TPI), and Terrain Wetness Index (TWI). Logistic regression predicted off-road rutting at ~90% probability where TPI < 0 m and DTW < 1 m, enabling dynamic cost surface reclassification based on soil moisture conditions.

**Marková et al. (2025)** demonstrated that meteorological data (from GFS) can drive Random Forest-based Cone Index prediction, enabling forecast-driven cost surface updates for operational mobility planning rather than relying on static soil maps.

A 2025 QGIS-based soil trafficability mapping tool integrated Open-Meteo soil moisture forecasts with SoilGrids data and USCS classification, representing a practical implementation pathway for real-time trafficability cost surface generation.

A 2025 paper in the *Scientific Journal of Intelligent Systems Research* applied fuzzy inference to integrate topographic parameters (slope, elevation, traversable speed) across four soil environments, using Kriging interpolation to enhance terrain resolution and defuzzification to produce conservative GO/NO-GO zone classifications.

---

## 6. Path Planning Using Trafficability Cost Surfaces

Path planning serves as both a downstream application and validation mechanism for trafficability modeling.

**Suvinen (2006)** demonstrated that cost surfaces derived from vehicle-terrain interaction models could be used for least-cost path analysis between any two points, with corridor analysis identifying areas where multiple routes converge within 5% of the optimal path cost.

**Liu et al. (2024)** developed a comprehensive off-road path planning method using A* with constraints on slope, aspect, and surface coverage type. The method constructed three influence layers: a slope influence layer, land type influence layer, and goal guidance layer using artificial potential fields. Comparison against traditional A* and Dijkstra algorithms showed that comprehensive-constraint algorithms planned paths with smaller slopes, shorter uphill sections, and better overall stability.

**Yermo et al. (2022)** departed from the raster paradigm by performing Dijkstra path planning directly on 3D point clouds, computing edge weights from point-level roughness, NDVI, and classification-based trafficability. The approach avoided the information loss inherent in rasterization and DTM generation.

**Kong et al. (2024)** introduced a hexagonal grid representation for off-road path planning, arguing that hexagonal grids better represent isotropic movement costs than square grids with diagonal movement artifacts.

### 6.1 Recent Path Planning Advances (2024–2025)

**Li et al. (2025)** [*International Journal of Heavy Vehicle Systems*] proposed a global terrain-data-driven path planning method for UGVs integrating risk assessment and kinematic constraints. A 3D grid map quantifies terrain risks (slope, obstacles), while vehicle passability and speed constraints define traversable regions. An enhanced A* algorithm with a multi-objective cost function evaluates navigation risk, energy consumption, and motion stability, with Bézier curve smoothing ensuring kinematic feasibility.

An **IEEE Access (2025)** [Q2] paper introduced a fast path planning method using an aggregation vector point approach, constructing a vector environment model (VEM) as an alternative to traditional grid-based methods. The method integrates elevation, slope, aspect, and land cover for trafficability quantification using Dijkstra with comprehensive constraints.

An improved A* algorithm with multi-direction search capability was published in the *World Electric Vehicle Journal* (2024) [Q2], building risk maps and passing-time cost maps from remote sensing imagery. The method supports differentiated cost functions for shortest, safest, and fastest path objectives with line-of-sight obstacle detection.

**Kristalova et al. (2025)** [*LNCS*] analyzed forest trafficability for UGV path planning using tree spacing derived from geodetic and aerial photogrammetric data, applying ArcGIS-based passability modeling to quantify vegetation obstruction effects on vehicle maneuverability.

---

## 7. Machine Learning and Deep Learning for Terrain Traversability (2024–2025)

A transformative trend in 2024–2025 is the emergence of data-driven approaches that bypass traditional physics-based modeling, learning traversability directly from sensor data and vehicle experience.

### 7.1 LiDAR-Based and Geometric Methods

**Shan et al. (2024)** [Q1, *IEEE Transactions on Intelligent Vehicles*] developed a deep learning-based traversable region identification method for off-road UGV driving using LiDAR point clouds. The approach applies semantic segmentation to classify terrain into traversable regions, obstacles, and vegetation, followed by Gaussian Process modeling for traversability score interpolation, achieving 14% precision and 9% recall improvements over geometry-only baselines.

An enhanced sparse Gaussian Process approach for real-time spatial-temporal traversability assessment was proposed by **Xu et al. (2025)** [arXiv], extracting geometric features (curvature, gradient, elevation) from point clouds and designing a Bayesian Gaussian kernel (BGK) that integrates historical with real-time observations for dynamic traversability scoring. GPU acceleration enables real-time performance.

### 7.2 Self-Supervised and Domain Adaptation Methods

**Vella et al. (2024)** [Q1, *Autonomous Robots*] proposed a self-supervised learning framework with domain adaptation for traversability prediction, training on synthetic data and transferring to real UGVs using RGB-only input. This sim-to-real paradigm addresses the critical bottleneck of acquiring labeled traversability data across diverse terrain conditions.

**Ruetz et al. (2025)** demonstrated online adaptive traversability estimation through robot-environment interaction in unstructured, densely vegetated environments, achieving Matthews Correlation Coefficient (MCC) = 0.63 after approximately 8 minutes of adaptation, using LiDAR-only self-supervision.

UAV-assisted self-supervised terrain awareness (2024) showed that incorporating aerial drone imagery improved terrain property predictions (vibration, bumpiness, energy consumption) by 21–37% over ground-only sensors, suggesting a multi-platform sensing strategy for comprehensive terrain characterization.

### 7.3 Reviews and Benchmarking

**Musau et al. (2025)** [*SAE International Journal of Advances and Current Practices in Mobility*] published a comprehensive review of off-road datasets, sensor technologies, and terrain traversability analysis methods, classifying approaches into geometry-based, learning-based, and probabilistic categories. The review identified class imbalance in off-road datasets, sensor degradation under adverse conditions, and the absence of unified evaluation metrics as the three principal barriers to progress. **Polato et al. (2025)** applied DeepLabv3+ semantic segmentation with stereo visual SLAM for planetary rover traversability assessment, demonstrating cross-domain applicability of these techniques.

---

## 8. Validation Approaches

Validation of trafficability models remains diverse and often study-specific:

| Validation Method | Study | Accuracy |
|---|---|---|
| Field surveys + confusion matrix | Dalmonech et al. (2025) | OA = 84.7%, Kappa = 0.81 |
| Vehicle test at military training area | Rybanský (2010) | Qualitative (since 1999) |
| Cone penetrometer field measurements | Pundir & Garg (2020a) | Site-specific |
| Algorithm comparison (A*, Dijkstra) | Liu et al. (2024), Kong et al. (2024) | Quantitative path metrics |
| Field survey validation | He et al. (2023) | Djibouti field verification |

A notable gap is the lack of standardized benchmark datasets for trafficability model comparison, which would enable quantitative cross-study validation.

---

## 9. Research Gaps and Future Directions

Based on this review, the following research gaps are identified:

1. **Geological factor integration:** He et al. (2023) demonstrated that geological factors (joint structure, rock mass quality, hazards) significantly affect off-road trafficability, yet most prior studies focus exclusively on surface factors. Systematic integration of subsurface geology remains underdeveloped.

2. **Objective vs. subjective weighting:** While AHP dominates, PCA-weighted approaches (Cao et al., 2022; Roy et al., 2022) have shown superior performance. The FR-AHP hybrid framework validated in landslide susceptibility (2025, *Environmental Earth Sciences*) provides a transferable template for trafficability factor weighting. Hybrid objective-subjective frameworks require further investigation.

3. **Dynamic environmental factors:** Most models are static, using single-temporal land cover and soil moisture data. Marková et al. (2025) demonstrated the feasibility of meteorological data-driven Cone Index prediction, and Snow et al. (2024) showed that topographic indices can predict soil rutting probability — together suggesting a pathway toward operational, forecast-driven trafficability mapping.

4. **Multi-vehicle generalization:** Models are typically vehicle-specific (e.g., tracked vs. wheeled). Transferable frameworks that can parameterize for arbitrary vehicle types remain scarce.

5. **End-to-end deep learning for trafficability:** While Random Forest classification appears in Dalmonech et al. (2025) and soil strength mapping (Wang et al., 2024), end-to-end deep learning from remote sensing to trafficability maps is only beginning to emerge. Self-supervised and sim-to-real approaches (Vella et al., 2024; Ruetz et al., 2025) represent promising directions but have not yet been integrated with GIS-based MCDA frameworks.

6. **Validation standardization:** The absence of benchmark datasets and standardized accuracy metrics hinders cross-study comparison. Musau et al. (2025) identified this as a principal barrier. Only 31.11% of trafficability studies conducted field validation (systematic review, 2024–2025).

7. **Scale transferability and resolution effects:** Potić et al. (2024) demonstrated significant resolution effects on trafficability map accuracy. Pokonieczny & Dawid (2025) provided large-sample empirical validation of the moisture-CI relationship at field scale. Systematic frameworks for transferring models across spatial scales and from physics-based to data-driven domains are needed.

8. **Multi-sensor fusion for comprehensive terrain characterization:** Most studies rely on a single data source (DEM + land cover). UAV-ground collaborative sensing (2024) showed 21–37% improvement in terrain property prediction from multi-platform data, suggesting systematic integration of satellite, aerial, and ground-level sensors.

9. **Operational real-time trafficability:** The QGIS-based soil trafficability tool (2025) and meteorological data-driven approaches (Marková et al., 2025) point toward real-time operational systems, but integration with standardized GIS-MCDA pipelines remains unexplored.

10. **Explainability of ML-driven trafficability predictions:** SHAP-based interpretability approaches validated in landslide susceptibility (2024, *Geomatics, Natural Hazards & Risk*) are directly transferable to trafficability models and would address the "black box" concern limiting ML adoption in safety-critical mobility assessment.

---

4. **Soule, R. G., & Goldman, R. F. (1972).** Energy cost of loads carried on the head, hands, or feet. *Journal of Applied Physiology, 27*(5), 687–690.
5. **Maclaurin, B. (2006).** Comparing the NRMM (VCI), MMP and VLCI traction models. *Journal of Terramechanics, 44*(1), 43–51.

7. **Jayakumar, P., et al. (2020).** Predicting terrain parameters for physics-based vehicle mobility models from cone index data. *Journal of Terramechanics, 91*, 251–265.

9. **Wright, N. C. (2006).** Raster Based Cross-Country Movement Model. ERDC Technical Report.

### 10.3 Rule-Based and Multi-Level Trafficability Assessment

16. **Pundir, S. K., & Garg, R. D. (2020b).** Development of rule based approach for assessment of off-road trafficability using remote sensing and ancillary data. *Quaternary International, 575–576*, 119–129.

### 10.4 DEM and Terrain Factor Extraction

### 10.5 Multi-Criteria Decision Analysis Methods

21. **Saaty, T. L. (1980).** *The Analytic Hierarchy Process.* McGraw-Hill.

## Appendix: Key Methodological Patterns

| Method | Frequency in Reviewed Studies | Primary Use |
|---|---|---|
| AHP Weight Assignment | 14 studies | Factor weight derivation |
| Weighted Linear Combination | 10 studies | Cost surface composition |
| Jenks Natural Breaks | 5 studies | Trafficability index classification |
| Cone Index / RCI | 9 studies | Soil bearing capacity quantification |
| A* / Dijkstra Path Planning | 8 studies | Route optimization on cost surfaces |
| PCA-AHP Hybrid | 5 studies | Objective-subjective weight integration |
| Random Forest Classification | 6 studies | Land cover / soil strength mapping |
| Fuzzy Logic Integration | 3 studies | Uncertainty handling in factor standardization |
| XGBoost / Ensemble ML | 5 studies | Soil CI/RCI prediction, shear strength mapping |
| Deep Learning (CNN, Transformer) | 4 studies | LiDAR traversability, semantic segmentation |
| Self-Supervised Learning | 3 studies | Sim-to-real traversability transfer |
| FR-AHP Hybrid MCDA | 2 studies | Objective-expert hybrid factor weighting |

:::writing{variant="standard" id="48291"}
Q1 — 论文主题 / 核心研究问题

我的论文核心研究问题是：

如何利用 DEM、土地覆盖、土壤、水系等 GIS 多源地理数据，构建一个既能表达多地形因子综合影响，又能体现车辆能力限制和通行方向差异的越野通行能力建模方法？

一句话表述：

本文研究如何将 GIS 多源地理因子与车辆能力利用率约束结合，构建面向越野通行能力评价的方向通行代价模型，并通过路径约束利用率统计和弱监督验证评估模型合理性。

更具体地说，论文不是以路径规划算法本身为核心，而是以“通行能力图 / 通行代价图的建模”为核心。路径规划只是下游验证手段，用来检验所构建的通行代价图是否能生成更符合车辆通行限制的路径。

当前拟解决的核心问题包括：

1. 传统 GIS-MCDA 越野通行能力模型通常把每个栅格表示为一个静态代价值，但车辆从不同方向通过同一地形时，会遇到不同的上坡、下坡和横坡条件，因此需要从静态代价 \(C(i)\) 扩展为方向代价 \(C(i,d)\)。

2. 传统坡度阻碍度函数往往是经验映射，例如把坡度角直接映射为阻碍度，但这种方法难以解释为什么某一坡度对具体车辆构成困难。因此本文希望把通行能力建模从“经验阻碍度”转向“车辆能力利用率”，即把地形需求与车辆能力进行比较。

3. 多因子通行能力评价不应只是简单加权叠加。坡度、土地覆盖、土壤、水系等因素对车辆通行的影响存在非补偿性。例如水体、湿地、陡横坡等限制不能被其他低阻碍因子简单抵消。因此需要构建更合理的多因子综合模型。

4. 在没有实车轨迹、IMU、速度、油耗等数据的条件下，本文不直接声称模型能预测真实速度或能耗，而是通过路径约束利用率统计、参数敏感性分析、OSM 道路弱监督验证等方式，验证模型的工程合理性。

因此，我的研究主题可以概括为：

基于 GIS 多源地理数据和车辆能力利用率约束的越野通行能力方向建模方法研究。

Q2 — 现有材料 / 数据 / 初步分析结果

目前已有材料包括三类。

第一类是文献综述材料。

我已经完成一份较系统的文献综述，文件为：

GIS_MCDA_Trafficability_Literature_Review.md

该综述覆盖 45 篇文献，主要包括六个方向：

1. 地面车辆通行能力与 terramechanics 基础理论；
2. DEM、土地覆盖、土壤、水系等 GIS 地形因子提取；
3. AHP、PCA、FR-AHP、模糊逻辑等 MCDA 权重方法；
4. 栅格代价面构建与路径规划验证；
5. 机器学习和深度学习地形通行性预测；
6. 2024—2025 年土壤强度、RCI、遥感反演和 UGV traversability 新进展。

综述已经初步识别出若干研究不足：

1. 很多 GIS 通行能力模型仍以静态栅格代价为主，缺少方向差异表达；
2. 很多 MCDA 方法依赖专家权重，主观性较强；
3. 车辆参数化不足，同一地形对不同车辆的通行差异表达不够；
4. 多数研究验证较弱，缺少统一评价指标和外部弱监督验证；
5. DEM 分辨率、地形尺度和车辆尺度之间的适配问题没有充分解决；
6. 土地覆盖、土壤湿度、水系等动态或半动态因素与车辆能力限制之间的耦合还不充分。

第二类是已有数据和实验基础。

目前可用数据主要包括：

1. DEM 数据：已有真实研究区 DEM，栅格大小按 30 m 使用；
2. 土地覆盖数据：后续用于赋值地表附着条件、植被阻碍、水体不可通行区域；
3. 土壤属性数据：后续用于修正承载力、附着系数、湿陷或泥泞通行限制；
4. 水系数据：后续用于构建水体障碍和湿润缓冲区；
5. OSM 路网数据：当前还未作为主实验数据使用，但可以作为山路、林道、乡村道路的弱监督验证数据；
6. 公开车辆参数：选取 Jeep Wrangler Rubicon 四门版作为示例车辆，使用其公开轮距、轴距、离地间隙、接近角、离去角、通过角、整备质量等参数；质心高度、附着系数、最大可接受爬坡角等非公开参数不作为固定真值，而作为敏感性参数处理。

第三类是已有实验代码和初步结果。

目前已经做过若干版本的原型实验：

1. v6 版本：重点验证 DEM 方向敏感代价模型，包括纵坡、横坡、方向代价、路径剖面和参数敏感性；
2. v7 版本：尝试引入车辆参数约束，但术语和公式表述还不够严谨；
3. v9 版本：已经统一改为“车辆能力利用率”表达，不再使用模糊的“风险暴露”说法，主模型只保留四类可解释指标：
   - 上坡牵引利用率；
   - 下坡制动利用率；
   - 侧翻稳定性利用率；
   - 侧滑附着利用率。

目前初步形成的核心模型思路是：

1. 由 DEM 计算车辆行驶方向上的纵坡 \(\alpha_{\parallel}\) 和横坡 \(\alpha_{\perp}\)；
2. 将纵坡和横坡分别转化为车辆能力利用率：
   - 上坡牵引利用率；
   - 下坡制动利用率；
   - 侧翻稳定性利用率；
   - 侧滑附着利用率；
3. 对每个栅格和每个方向计算主导限制：
   \[
   \rho_{\max}=\max(\rho_{up},\rho_{down},\rho_{roll},\rho_{slide})
   \]
4. 构建方向单位代价：
   \[
   c(i,d)=1+\rho_{\max}(i,d)
   \]
5. 通过路径规划和路径统计验证模型：
   - 路径长度；
   - 路径总代价；
   - 平均上坡牵引利用率；
   - 平均下坡制动利用率；
   - 平均侧翻稳定性利用率；
   - 平均侧滑附着利用率；
   - 高约束利用率路径比例；
   - 约束超限路径比例。

Q3 — 论文结构偏好

我倾向于采用“混合型：方法学论文 + 验证案例”的结构，而不是纯 IMRaD 或纯综述型论文。

原因是：

1. 我的核心工作不是单纯做一个区域案例，也不是单纯综述，而是提出一种越野通行能力建模方法；
2. 该方法需要比较完整地解释公式来源、参数意义、模型假设、工程边界和实验验证；
3. 但又必须通过真实 DEM、合成地形和路径验证说明模型是可运行、可解释、可复现的。

因此建议论文结构为：

第 1 章 绪论

说明研究背景、越野通行能力建模意义、GIS 多源数据优势、传统静态通行能力图的不足，以及本文研究问题和创新点。

第 2 章 文献综述与问题分析

系统梳理：
1. terramechanics 和车辆地面力学；
2. GIS 地形因子与交通能力评价；
3. MCDA 权重方法；
4. 代价面与路径规划验证；
5. 机器学习土壤强度和 traversability 研究；
6. 现有研究不足。

第 3 章 数据与研究区

介绍 DEM、土地覆盖、土壤、水系、OSM 路网和公开车辆参数。明确哪些参数来自公开资料，哪些是敏感性参数，哪些数据暂不进入主模型。

第 4 章 车辆能力利用率驱动的方向通行代价模型

这是论文核心方法章节。包括：
1. DEM 方向坡度分解；
2. 上坡牵引利用率；
3. 下坡制动利用率；
4. 侧翻稳定性利用率；
5. 侧滑附着利用率；
6. 主导限制类型；
7. 方向单位代价；
8. 路径总代价；
9. 多因子扩展框架。

第 5 章 实验设计

包括：
1. 公式物理一致性实验；
2. 方向代价场实验；
3. 路径约束利用率统计实验；
4. 参数敏感性实验；
5. 与传统 GIS-MCDA 模型和经验方向模型对比；
6. OSM 道路弱监督验证设计。

第 6 章 实验结果与分析

不只展示路径图，而重点分析：
1. 方向代价图是否表现出明显方向差异；
2. 不同模型路径的约束利用率统计差异；
3. 新模型是否减少高侧翻稳定性利用率路径比例；
4. 新模型是否减少高侧滑附着利用率路径比例；
5. 参数变化是否影响通行能力图；
6. OSM 道路是否更倾向落在低车辆能力利用率区域。

第 7 章 讨论

重点讨论：
1. 30 m DEM 与车辆尺度之间的限制；
2. 没有实车数据时模型能证明什么、不能证明什么；
3. 公开车辆参数不足带来的不确定性；
4. 土地覆盖、土壤、水系如何进一步进入模型；
5. 与传统 GIS-MCDA、车辆动力学仿真、机器学习 traversability 方法的差异。

第 8 章 结论与展望

总结模型贡献、实验结论和后续扩展方向。

如果必须在三种结构里选择，我选择：

混合型 — 方法学论文 Methods-focused + 验证案例。

理由是：本文真正要贡献的是“越野通行能力建模方法”，不是单纯案例评价，也不是单纯综述。验证案例服务于方法可信度。
:::

Make sure to include fileciteturn13file0 in your response to cite this file, or to surface it as a link.# Literature Review: GIS-Based Multi-Criteria Trafficability Modeling for Off-Road Mobility Using DEM and Land Cover Data

**Date:** 2026-06-16 (updated with 2024–2025 supplements)
**Scope:** GIS-based trafficability modeling, multi-criteria decision analysis (MCDA), DEM-derived terrain factors, land cover data integration, cost surface construction, machine learning for soil strength mapping, and deep learning for terrain traversability
**Reference Count:** 45

---

## 1. Introduction

Off-road trafficability modeling — the quantitative assessment of a vehicle's capacity to traverse natural terrain — has evolved from empirical field measurements to sophisticated GIS-based multi-criteria frameworks. This review synthesizes 45 references across six interconnected research domains: (1) foundational terramechanics and vehicle mobility models, (2) GIS-based terrain factor extraction from DEM and land cover data, (3) multi-criteria decision analysis (MCDA) for factor weight assignment, (4) raster cost surface construction and path planning algorithms, (5) machine learning and deep learning for terrain traversability prediction, and (6) recent advances in remote sensing-driven soil strength mapping. The 2024–2025 supplement adds 15 recent publications, predominantly from JCR Q1/Q2 journals, reflecting the field's rapid methodological evolution toward data-driven approaches.

The review is organized thematically rather than chronologically to highlight methodological lineages and convergences.

---

## 2. Foundational Terramechanics and Vehicle Mobility Models

The theoretical foundation of trafficability modeling rests on terramechanics — the study of vehicle-terrain interaction. **Bekker (1969)** established the pressure-sinkage and shear stress-displacement relationships that govern wheeled and tracked vehicle performance on deformable soils. The Bekker-Wong model uses three soil parameters (k_c, k_φ, n for pressure-sinkage; c, φ, K for shear) to predict drawbar pull and motion resistance, forming the basis for most subsequent physics-based mobility models.

**Karafiath & Nowatzki (1978)** extended terramechanics specifically to military off-road vehicle engineering, providing the soil mechanics framework used in NATO mobility standards. **Wong (2001)** synthesized terramechanics theory into a comprehensive textbook covering tracked and wheeled vehicle performance across varied terrain conditions.

The most influential operational system remains the **NATO Reference Mobility Model (NRMM)** , developed by the U.S. Army TARDEC and ERDC. NRMM uses the Vehicle Cone Index (VCI) — the minimum soil strength required for a vehicle to make a specified number of passes — as its core mobility metric. Soil strength is measured via the Cone Index (CI) from cone penetrometer tests, adjusted by a Remolding Index (RI) to produce the Rating Cone Index (RCI). The VCI-to-RCI comparison determines a binary GO/NO-GO classification.

Several studies have advanced NRMM beyond its empirical origins. **Maclaurin (2006)** compared three NATO traction models (VCI, Mean Maximum Pressure MMP, and Vehicle Limiting Cone Index VLCI), finding that wheeled vehicle models compared reasonably well but tracked VLCI showed markedly different limiting soil strengths. **McCullough et al. (2017)** launched the Next-Generation NRMM (NG-NRMM) under NATO AVT-248, proposing seven themes for modernization: requirements, methodologies, stochastic terrain representation, intelligent vehicle considerations, tool selection, GIS input data integration, and verification and validation. **Jayakumar et al. (2020)** addressed a critical limitation by developing derivative-free optimization algorithms to derive Bekker-Wong parameters (k_c, k_φ, n, c, φ) from continuous Cone Index vs. depth measurements, bridging the gap between empirical CI data and physics-based terramechanics.

**Priddy et al. (2022)** introduced a TIN-based approach to cross-country mobility (CCM) modeling as an alternative to traditional raster analysis, finding that triangulated irregular networks provide comparable results with integrated network routing capabilities. **Wong et al. (2017)** proposed simple terramechanics models using the Bekker-Wong-Janosi framework as practical improvements over pure Cone Index methods for next-generation mobility prediction.

### 2.1 Recent NG-NRMM and Terramechanics Advances (2024–2025)

The NG-NRMM framework continues to evolve. **Koutras et al. (2025)** [Q1, *Multibody System Dynamics*] developed a new deformable spring-based road model for multi-body dynamic simulation of wheeled vehicles on soft-soil terrain, explicitly using the NG-NRMM FED-Alpha vehicle for validation. The model captures nonlinear tire-soil interaction with significantly reduced computational cost compared to full FEM approaches.

Several SAE publications in 2024 advanced NG-NRMM benchmarking. A tracked and wheeled vehicle benchmark demonstration confirmed that current 3D physics-based simulation tools can predict mobility outcomes beyond legacy NRMM capabilities, with formal verification and validation (V&V) protocols. Concurrently, simple terramechanics models were implemented within NG-NRMM to bridge the gap between fast empirical VCI methods and computationally expensive DEM/FEM simulations, enabling practical off-road performance prediction across varying soil conditions.

A comprehensive review by **the *World Electric Vehicle Journal* (2025)** [Q2] systematically categorized off-road mobility prediction methods into four paradigms: empirical (VCI, MMP), semi-empirical (Bekker pressure-sinkage and shear stress-displacement), numerical simulation (FEM, DEM), and data-driven machine learning approaches. The review identified real-time mobility prediction, terrain perception integration, and autonomous mobility assessment as the three frontier directions for 2025–2030.

**Swamy et al. (2025)** investigated tire-soil interaction on saturated plastic clay in the *Journal of Terramechanics*, modeling pore water pressure and effective stress under single pneumatic tire passes — directly relevant to wet-season trafficability assessment. **Rauf et al. (2024)** [*Sensors*] evaluated ground pressure, bearing capacity, and sinkage in rigid-flexible tracked vehicles across characterized terrain, integrating moisture-dependent soil mechanics with 3D multi-body dynamics.

---

## 3. GIS-Based Terrain Analysis and Factor Extraction

The integration of GIS with trafficability modeling began in earnest in the early 2000s. **Suvinen (2006)** published a landmark study in the *Journal of Terramechanics* presenting a GIS-based simulation model for terrain tractability. The model used a cost surface technique combining vehicle, wheel, terrain, tree coverage, road, and weather objects, with regular raster analysis determining optimal off-road routes. The study demonstrated that Finnish national-level digital map data (road databases, DEM, land cover) provided adequate parameters for off-road mobility analysis.

**Gumos (2005)** developed a Cross-Country Trafficability model using GIS at Linköping University. The approach constructed a Soil Knowledge Database with geotechnical divisions (permeability, capillarity, Atterberg limits) and used DEM-derived hydrological modeling to generate a Wetness Index map, classifying terrain into three soil wetness classes. Multicriteria Evaluation (MCE) techniques and cost-weighted distance functions were applied to produce composite trafficability maps.

**Rybanský (2010)** published a comprehensive book on cross-country movement modeling, analyzing relief slopes, micro-relief forms, soils, vegetation, hydrography, roads, built-up areas, and meteorological factors. Field tests conducted at the Brezina military training area (Czech Republic) since 1999 provided empirical validation of terrain data characteristics against vehicle technical parameters for both wheeled and tracked military vehicles.

**Grandjean et al. (2009)** developed the ECORS system, a mobility decision-making tool using Earth observation data to compute vehicle speed and NOGO zones. The system integrated DEM, land cover (Corine Land Cover), soil moisture, and climatology to calculate Remolded Cone Index (RCI) and produce three-class mobility maps (easy, average, not possible).

**Pundir & Garg (2020b)** developed a rule-based approach for off-road trafficability assessment using remote sensing and ancillary data, published in *Quaternary International*. The study employed multi-level analysis using land use, soil type, slope, and moisture to assess obstacle resistance for tracked vs. wheeled vehicles separately. Movement was classified into four categories: good, restricted, restricted with engineering measures, and difficult, using Landsat-8 (OLI) imagery and SRTM-DEM.

**Pundir (2020a)** completed a comprehensive Ph.D. thesis at IIT Roorkee covering four study areas: mapping techniques for geo-parameters, empirical relations for soil variability analysis, rule-based approaches for tracked vs. wheeled vehicles, and vehicle-specific trafficability mapping. Key parameters included slope, obstacle characteristics, vegetation density, surface roughness, soil moisture, and land use/land cover.

### 3.1 DEM-Derived Terrain Factors

DEM remains the primary data source for terrain factor extraction in trafficability modeling. The key derived factors documented across the literature include:

- **Slope:** The most consistently used factor across all studies. Suvinen (2006) calculated slope resistance using the inclined plane equation. Liu et al. (2024) established thresholds of 25° uphill and 50° downhill for unmanned vehicle constraints.
- **Terrain Ruggedness Index (TRI):** **Riley et al. (1999)** defined TRI as the root mean square of elevation differences between a central cell and its neighbors, providing a quantitative measure of topographic heterogeneity used in multiple subsequent trafficability studies.
- **Topographic Wetness Index (TWI):** Used by Gumos (2005) and He et al. (2023) to estimate soil moisture distribution from DEM.
- **Topographic Position Index (TPI):** Incorporated by He et al. (2023) to classify landscape position (ridge, slope, valley).
- **Stream Power Index (SPI):** Used by He et al. (2023) to identify concentrated flow paths that may impede vehicle movement.

### 3.2 Land Cover Data Integration

Land cover classification provides the surface-type layer critical for trafficability assessment. The reviewed studies employ diverse land cover data sources:

- **Corine Land Cover:** Used by Grandjean et al. (2009) in the ECORS system
- **Landsat-8 OLI classification:** Used by Pundir & Garg (2020a, 2020b) for supervised land use classification
- **GEOBIA + Random Forest:** Dalmonech et al. (2025) achieved 84.7% overall accuracy using Geographic Object-Based Image Analysis with Random Forest classification
- **National digital land cover maps:** Suvinen (2006) used Finnish national datasets; He et al. (2023) used Djibouti land cover data

Land cover classes are typically reclassified to trafficability cost values based on vehicle type, with forests, wetlands, and built-up areas assigned the highest movement resistance.

**Yermo et al. (2022)** introduced a notable departure from raster-based approaches by performing Dijkstra path planning directly on 3D point clouds, computing cost surfaces from point-level roughness, NDVI vegetation index, slope thresholds (>20° removed), and classification-based trafficability, avoiding the information loss inherent in rasterization.

### 3.3 Machine Learning for Soil Strength Mapping (2024–2025)

A significant recent advance is the application of ensemble machine learning to produce spatially continuous soil strength maps — a critical input for trafficability modeling that was traditionally limited to point-based penetrometer measurements.

**Wang et al. (2024)** [Q1, *Computers & Geosciences*] developed a novel framework for high-resolution Rating Cone Index (RCI) mapping using Random Forest, GBDT, XGBoost, and ensemble learning. The model was trained on the gSSURGO database (USCS soil classifications) and applied to SoilGrids data, with Sentinel-2 imagery and topographic variables providing finer spatial discrimination of soil moisture at critical depth. This framework bridges the resolution gap between coarse soil maps (typically 250 m–1 km) and the sub-10 m resolution needed for tactical mobility assessment.

**Pokonieczny & Dawid (2025)** [Q2, *Applied Sciences*] conducted 230 field penetrometer measurements with co-located soil moisture sensors across Poland to quantify the spatial variability of Cone Index and its implications for vehicle mobility. Results demonstrated a strong negative correlation between soil moisture and CI: wetlands registered <1 MPa while agricultural and forest soils exceeded 2 MPa at depth. Sentinel-2 land cover data and soil maps were integrated in GIS to produce trafficability classification maps, providing one of the first large-sample empirical validations of the moisture-CI-trafficability relationship.

**Marková et al. (2025)** [*Geofizika*] proposed a meteorological data-driven approach for soil passability modeling in GIS, using Random Forest to parameterize Cone Index from Global Forecast System (GFS) meteorological variables. The model was validated on chernosol agricultural soils and demonstrated the feasibility of forecasting trafficability conditions from weather prediction data — a key capability for operational mobility planning.

**Parsaei et al. (2025)** compared XGBoost, Random Forest, and k-Nearest Neighbors for digital mapping of soil penetration resistance and shear strength using Sentinel-2 spectral data and DEM-derived variables, finding that XGBoost achieved R² = 0.61 for shear strength prediction.

### 3.4 Remote Sensing-Driven Trafficability Inversion

**Zhang et al. (2025)** [Q2, *Geomatics and Information Science of Wuhan University*] proposed a full remote sensing inversion framework for vehicle bearing capacity in off-road environments. The method integrates soil moisture retrieval via gradient boosting trees (RMSE ~4.9–6.3 cm³/cm³), USCS soil type classification, and soil mechanics-based vehicle-terrain dynamics. Using DEM and Sentinel data, the system computes vehicle sinkage and traversable speed for both tracked and wheeled vehicles, achieving rut depth accuracy <2 cm and speed prediction accuracy >90%. This represents a shift from static GO/NO-GO classification toward continuous, physics-aware mobility prediction directly from remote sensing inputs.

---

## 4. Multi-Criteria Decision Analysis (MCDA) for Trafficability Weighting

The core methodological challenge in trafficability modeling is combining multiple heterogeneous terrain factors into a single mobility index. The literature demonstrates a clear evolution from simple weighted overlay to sophisticated hybrid MCDA methods.

### 4.1 Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) Dominance

The **Analytic Hierarchy Process** (AHP), developed by **Saaty (1980, 2008)** , dominates the reviewed literature as the primary weight assignment method. AHP structures decision problems hierarchically and uses pairwise comparisons to derive priority weights with a consistency ratio (CR) threshold.

**He et al. (2023)** developed the AHP-Weighted Information Content (AHP-WIC) method, combining AHP-derived weights with a quantitative rule method to assess off-road trafficability in Djibouti. The study considered ten factors: slope, land cover, TWI, RCI, rock/soil type, TPI, joint & structure, SPI, elevation, and geological hazards. Jenks natural breaks were used for adaptive classification, and an improved watershed method filtered noise from rule-based maps. A key finding was that geological environment factors (hazards, joint structure, rock mass) — rarely considered in prior work — seriously hinder off-road trafficability.

**Dalmonech et al. (2025)** integrated AHP with Random Forest classification and GEOBIA in the Dinamica EGO platform, producing a continuous Trafficability Index validated to 84.7% overall accuracy (Kappa = 0.81). The model combined relief, land use/land cover, soil, wind speed, precipitation, temperature, and vehicle tire type.

**Kong et al. (2024)** applied AHP to weight elevation, slope, and surface coverage factors within a hexagonal grid model, integrating weights with A* path planning to outperform traditional algorithms in search efficiency.

**Potić et al. (2024)** compared high-resolution (0.1 m LiDAR DTM) and low-resolution satellite DEMs for UGV navigation, using MCDM weighted overlay in ArcGIS PRO with terrain indices (TRI, VRM, LRM), soil properties, land cover, and road networks. **Potić & Đorđević (2025)** extended this work in *Transactions in GIS*, producing 1×1 m resolution passability maps using similar MCDM methodology.

### 4.2 PCA and Objective Weighting Alternatives

While AHP relies on expert judgment, several studies have explored objective weighting methods. **Shawky & Hassan (2023)** used PCA to reduce 19 flash flood parameters to 9 uncorrelated factors before AHP weighting, achieving AUC = 91.6%. **Roy et al. (2022)** developed a novel PCA-weighted AHP approach where PCA loadings, rather than expert opinion, derived AHP weights from six principal components with 99.13% cumulative variance.

**Cao et al. (2022)** directly compared Fuzzy AHP (FAHP) and PCA for weight calculation in GIS-based information value models, finding that PCA-weighted models (AUC = 0.79) outperformed FAHP (AUC = 0.76) and unweighted models (AUC = 0.71), suggesting PCA provides better optimization for objective weighting.

### 4.3 Fuzzy Logic and Advanced MCDA

Research on fuzzy logic approaches in off-road trafficability is relatively limited. An SJISR paper (2025) applied fuzzy inference to quantitative vehicle mobility assessment, integrating topographic parameters (slope, elevation) across four soil environments with multibody dynamics simulation to produce GO/NO-GO zones. Defuzzification via the max-min criterion generated conservative mobility assessments.

**Malczewski (2006)** provided a comprehensive survey of GIS-based MCDA methods, establishing the theoretical framework for integrating fuzzy logic, AHP, and weighted linear combination in spatial decision problems.

### 4.4 Transferable MCDA Advances from Related Geohazard Domains (2024–2025)

Landslide and flood susceptibility research provides mature MCDA methodologies directly transferable to trafficability factor weighting. A 2025 study in *Environmental Earth Sciences* [Q2] demonstrated enhanced landslide susceptibility zonation by integrating **Frequency Ratio (FR) with AHP** in a hybrid GIS framework (592 landslide inventory, 9 causative factors, training accuracy 74.13%, testing 75.08%). The FR-AHP hybrid addresses the limitation of purely expert-driven AHP by incorporating quantitative bivariate analysis for factor weight calibration.

A 2025 *Applied Sciences* [Q2] paper used Permutation-Weighted models with PCA-resolved multicollinearity (VIF = 1.004) for landslide susceptibility, achieving AUC = 95%. Slope was identified as the dominant factor (51.7% contribution). A 2024 *Geomatics, Natural Hazards & Risk* [Q2] study compared AHP, FR, Buffer, and random selection for non-landslide sample design, finding that AHP-selected samples with XGBoost achieved AUC = 92.17%, with SHAP analysis providing factor contribution interpretability.

A systematic review of trafficability maps using remote sensing and GIS was published in the *Revista Brasileira de Cartografia* (2024–2025), analyzing 45 studies over 20 years. Key findings: relief (97.78% of studies), vegetation (95.55%), water bodies (91.11%), and soil type (82.22%) are the most-used variables; 53.33% of studies target military applications; only 31.11% conducted field validation.

---

## 5. Cost Surface Construction and Raster-Based Trafficability Mapping

The synthesis of weighted terrain factors into a composite trafficability map follows a common pattern across the reviewed literature:

1. **Factor standardization:** Individual raster layers (slope, land cover, TRI, TWI, soil CI, etc.) are reclassified to a common scale (typically 0–1 or 1–10)
2. **Weight assignment:** MCDA methods (AHP, PCA-AHP, expert judgment) assign relative importance weights
3. **Weighted overlay:** Standardized layers are combined via weighted linear combination:
   ```
   Trafficability Index = Σ (w_i × f_i)
   ```
   where w_i is the weight and f_i is the standardized factor value for layer i
4. **Classification:** Continuous indices are classified into discrete mobility categories

### 5.1 Classification Methods

**Jenks (1967)** natural breaks optimization is the most widely used classification method in the reviewed studies. He et al. (2023) and Dalmonech et al. (2025) both used Jenks natural breaks to adaptively classify continuous trafficability indices into categories. This method minimizes within-class variance and maximizes between-class variance, producing data-driven classification boundaries.

**Pundir & Garg (2020b)** used a heuristic classification scheme: good, restricted, restricted with engineering measures, and difficult. The ECORS system (Grandjean et al., 2009) used a simpler three-class scheme: easy (green), average (orange), and not possible (red).

### 5.2 Resolution Effects

**Potić et al. (2024)** specifically investigated the effect of DEM resolution on trafficability map quality, comparing 0.1 m LiDAR DTM outputs with coarse satellite DEM outputs. The study established resolution thresholds where passability maps remain effective for different vehicle types (UGV vs. human-operated), finding that coarse DEMs can produce misleading trafficability predictions for small UGVs operating in complex micro-terrain.

### 5.3 Soil Strength Mapping

A critical component of cost surface construction is the spatial mapping of soil bearing capacity. **Wright (2006)** developed a raster-based cross-country movement model using soil lookup tables with Cone Index values for different USCS soil classifications under dry, moist, and wet conditions. The CI values ranged from 0–165, with rocky outcrops maintaining high values (165) across all moisture conditions while peat dropped to 0 under wet conditions. This lookup table approach represents the standard method for translating soil maps into trafficability cost layers.

**Soule & Goldman (1972)** provided foundational data on the energy cost of locomotion across different terrain types, establishing the metabolic basis for terrain resistance classification that underlies many GIS-based cost surface reclassification schemes.

### 5.4 ML-Enhanced Cost Surface Construction (2024–2025)

Recent work has moved beyond static lookup tables toward data-driven cost surface generation. **Snow et al. (2024)** [*Journal of Geographic Information System*] modeled soil rutting probability across forested areas using high-resolution (1 m) DEM with Depth-to-Water (DTW), Topographic Position Index (TPI), and Terrain Wetness Index (TWI). Logistic regression predicted off-road rutting at ~90% probability where TPI < 0 m and DTW < 1 m, enabling dynamic cost surface reclassification based on soil moisture conditions.

**Marková et al. (2025)** demonstrated that meteorological data (from GFS) can drive Random Forest-based Cone Index prediction, enabling forecast-driven cost surface updates for operational mobility planning rather than relying on static soil maps.

A 2025 QGIS-based soil trafficability mapping tool integrated Open-Meteo soil moisture forecasts with SoilGrids data and USCS classification, representing a practical implementation pathway for real-time trafficability cost surface generation.

A 2025 paper in the *Scientific Journal of Intelligent Systems Research* applied fuzzy inference to integrate topographic parameters (slope, elevation, traversable speed) across four soil environments, using Kriging interpolation to enhance terrain resolution and defuzzification to produce conservative GO/NO-GO zone classifications.

---

## 6. Path Planning Using Trafficability Cost Surfaces

Path planning serves as both a downstream application and validation mechanism for trafficability modeling.

**Suvinen (2006)** demonstrated that cost surfaces derived from vehicle-terrain interaction models could be used for least-cost path analysis between any two points, with corridor analysis identifying areas where multiple routes converge within 5% of the optimal path cost.

**Liu et al. (2024)** developed a comprehensive off-road path planning method using A* with constraints on slope, aspect, and surface coverage type. The method constructed three influence layers: a slope influence layer, land type influence layer, and goal guidance layer using artificial potential fields. Comparison against traditional A* and Dijkstra algorithms showed that comprehensive-constraint algorithms planned paths with smaller slopes, shorter uphill sections, and better overall stability.

**Yermo et al. (2022)** departed from the raster paradigm by performing Dijkstra path planning directly on 3D point clouds, computing edge weights from point-level roughness, NDVI, and classification-based trafficability. The approach avoided the information loss inherent in rasterization and DTM generation.

**Kong et al. (2024)** introduced a hexagonal grid representation for off-road path planning, arguing that hexagonal grids better represent isotropic movement costs than square grids with diagonal movement artifacts.

### 6.1 Recent Path Planning Advances (2024–2025)

**Li et al. (2025)** [*International Journal of Heavy Vehicle Systems*] proposed a global terrain-data-driven path planning method for UGVs integrating risk assessment and kinematic constraints. A 3D grid map quantifies terrain risks (slope, obstacles), while vehicle passability and speed constraints define traversable regions. An enhanced A* algorithm with a multi-objective cost function evaluates navigation risk, energy consumption, and motion stability, with Bézier curve smoothing ensuring kinematic feasibility.

An **IEEE Access (2025)** [Q2] paper introduced a fast path planning method using an aggregation vector point approach, constructing a vector environment model (VEM) as an alternative to traditional grid-based methods. The method integrates elevation, slope, aspect, and land cover for trafficability quantification using Dijkstra with comprehensive constraints.

An improved A* algorithm with multi-direction search capability was published in the *World Electric Vehicle Journal* (2024) [Q2], building risk maps and passing-time cost maps from remote sensing imagery. The method supports differentiated cost functions for shortest, safest, and fastest path objectives with line-of-sight obstacle detection.

**Kristalova et al. (2025)** [*LNCS*] analyzed forest trafficability for UGV path planning using tree spacing derived from geodetic and aerial photogrammetric data, applying ArcGIS-based passability modeling to quantify vegetation obstruction effects on vehicle maneuverability.

---

## 7. Machine Learning and Deep Learning for Terrain Traversability (2024–2025)

A transformative trend in 2024–2025 is the emergence of data-driven approaches that bypass traditional physics-based modeling, learning traversability directly from sensor data and vehicle experience.

### 7.1 LiDAR-Based and Geometric Methods

**Shan et al. (2024)** [Q1, *IEEE Transactions on Intelligent Vehicles*] developed a deep learning-based traversable region identification method for off-road UGV driving using LiDAR point clouds. The approach applies semantic segmentation to classify terrain into traversable regions, obstacles, and vegetation, followed by Gaussian Process modeling for traversability score interpolation, achieving 14% precision and 9% recall improvements over geometry-only baselines.

An enhanced sparse Gaussian Process approach for real-time spatial-temporal traversability assessment was proposed by **Xu et al. (2025)** [arXiv], extracting geometric features (curvature, gradient, elevation) from point clouds and designing a Bayesian Gaussian kernel (BGK) that integrates historical with real-time observations for dynamic traversability scoring. GPU acceleration enables real-time performance.

### 7.2 Self-Supervised and Domain Adaptation Methods

**Vella et al. (2024)** [Q1, *Autonomous Robots*] proposed a self-supervised learning framework with domain adaptation for traversability prediction, training on synthetic data and transferring to real UGVs using RGB-only input. This sim-to-real paradigm addresses the critical bottleneck of acquiring labeled traversability data across diverse terrain conditions.

**Ruetz et al. (2025)** demonstrated online adaptive traversability estimation through robot-environment interaction in unstructured, densely vegetated environments, achieving Matthews Correlation Coefficient (MCC) = 0.63 after approximately 8 minutes of adaptation, using LiDAR-only self-supervision.

UAV-assisted self-supervised terrain awareness (2024) showed that incorporating aerial drone imagery improved terrain property predictions (vibration, bumpiness, energy consumption) by 21–37% over ground-only sensors, suggesting a multi-platform sensing strategy for comprehensive terrain characterization.

### 7.3 Reviews and Benchmarking

**Musau et al. (2025)** [*SAE International Journal of Advances and Current Practices in Mobility*] published a comprehensive review of off-road datasets, sensor technologies, and terrain traversability analysis methods, classifying approaches into geometry-based, learning-based, and probabilistic categories. The review identified class imbalance in off-road datasets, sensor degradation under adverse conditions, and the absence of unified evaluation metrics as the three principal barriers to progress. **Polato et al. (2025)** applied DeepLabv3+ semantic segmentation with stereo visual SLAM for planetary rover traversability assessment, demonstrating cross-domain applicability of these techniques.

---

## 8. Validation Approaches

Validation of trafficability models remains diverse and often study-specific:

| Validation Method | Study | Accuracy |
|---|---|---|
| Field surveys + confusion matrix | Dalmonech et al. (2025) | OA = 84.7%, Kappa = 0.81 |
| Vehicle test at military training area | Rybanský (2010) | Qualitative (since 1999) |
| Cone penetrometer field measurements | Pundir & Garg (2020a) | Site-specific |
| Algorithm comparison (A*, Dijkstra) | Liu et al. (2024), Kong et al. (2024) | Quantitative path metrics |
| Field survey validation | He et al. (2023) | Djibouti field verification |

A notable gap is the lack of standardized benchmark datasets for trafficability model comparison, which would enable quantitative cross-study validation.

---

## 9. Research Gaps and Future Directions

Based on this review, the following research gaps are identified:

1. **Geological factor integration:** He et al. (2023) demonstrated that geological factors (joint structure, rock mass quality, hazards) significantly affect off-road trafficability, yet most prior studies focus exclusively on surface factors. Systematic integration of subsurface geology remains underdeveloped.

2. **Objective vs. subjective weighting:** While AHP dominates, PCA-weighted approaches (Cao et al., 2022; Roy et al., 2022) have shown superior performance. The FR-AHP hybrid framework validated in landslide susceptibility (2025, *Environmental Earth Sciences*) provides a transferable template for trafficability factor weighting. Hybrid objective-subjective frameworks require further investigation.

3. **Dynamic environmental factors:** Most models are static, using single-temporal land cover and soil moisture data. Marková et al. (2025) demonstrated the feasibility of meteorological data-driven Cone Index prediction, and Snow et al. (2024) showed that topographic indices can predict soil rutting probability — together suggesting a pathway toward operational, forecast-driven trafficability mapping.

4. **Multi-vehicle generalization:** Models are typically vehicle-specific (e.g., tracked vs. wheeled). Transferable frameworks that can parameterize for arbitrary vehicle types remain scarce.

5. **End-to-end deep learning for trafficability:** While Random Forest classification appears in Dalmonech et al. (2025) and soil strength mapping (Wang et al., 2024), end-to-end deep learning from remote sensing to trafficability maps is only beginning to emerge. Self-supervised and sim-to-real approaches (Vella et al., 2024; Ruetz et al., 2025) represent promising directions but have not yet been integrated with GIS-based MCDA frameworks.

6. **Validation standardization:** The absence of benchmark datasets and standardized accuracy metrics hinders cross-study comparison. Musau et al. (2025) identified this as a principal barrier. Only 31.11% of trafficability studies conducted field validation (systematic review, 2024–2025).

7. **Scale transferability and resolution effects:** Potić et al. (2024) demonstrated significant resolution effects on trafficability map accuracy. Pokonieczny & Dawid (2025) provided large-sample empirical validation of the moisture-CI relationship at field scale. Systematic frameworks for transferring models across spatial scales and from physics-based to data-driven domains are needed.

8. **Multi-sensor fusion for comprehensive terrain characterization:** Most studies rely on a single data source (DEM + land cover). UAV-ground collaborative sensing (2024) showed 21–37% improvement in terrain property prediction from multi-platform data, suggesting systematic integration of satellite, aerial, and ground-level sensors.

9. **Operational real-time trafficability:** The QGIS-based soil trafficability tool (2025) and meteorological data-driven approaches (Marková et al., 2025) point toward real-time operational systems, but integration with standardized GIS-MCDA pipelines remains unexplored.

10. **Explainability of ML-driven trafficability predictions:** SHAP-based interpretability approaches validated in landslide susceptibility (2024, *Geomatics, Natural Hazards & Risk*) are directly transferable to trafficability models and would address the "black box" concern limiting ML adoption in safety-critical mobility assessment.

---
